<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">NEXRAD</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LabNexrad.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/LabNexrad.jpg/220px-LabNexrad.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/LabNexrad.jpg/330px-LabNexrad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/LabNexrad.jpg/440px-LabNexrad.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LabNexrad.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
NEXRAD Radar at the WSR-88D <a href="/wiki/Radar_Operations_Center" title="Radar Operations Center">Radar Operations Center</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wea03317_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1099" data-file-width="1763" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Wea03317_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg/220px-Wea03317_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Wea03317_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg/330px-Wea03317_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Wea03317_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg/440px-Wea03317_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wea03317_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Testbed of the WSR-88D on display at the <a href="/wiki/National_Severe_Storms_Laboratory" title="National Severe Storms Laboratory">National Severe Storms Laboratory</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>NEXRAD</b> or <b>Nexrad</b> (<b><u>Nex</u></b>t-Generation <b><u>Rad</u></b>ar) is a network of 159 high-resolution <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S-band" title="S-band">S-band</a> <a href="/wiki/Pulse-Doppler_radar" title="Pulse-Doppler radar">Doppler</a> <a href="/wiki/Weather_radar" title="Weather radar">weather radars</a> operated by the <a href="/wiki/National_Weather_Service" title="National Weather Service">National Weather Service</a> (NWS), an agency of the <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration</a> (NOAA) within the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Commerce" title="United States Department of Commerce">United States Department of Commerce</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aviation_Administration" title="Federal Aviation Administration">Federal Aviation Administration</a> (FAA) within the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Transportation" title="United States Department of Transportation">Department of Transportation</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">U.S. Air Force</a> within the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">Department of Defense</a>. Its technical name is <b>WSR-88D</b>, which stands for <u>W</u>eather <u>S</u>urveillance <u>R</u>adar, 19<u>88</u>, <u>D</u>oppler.</p>
<p>NEXRAD detects <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">atmospheric</a> movement or <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">wind</a>. It returns <a href="/wiki/Data" title="Data">data</a> which when processed can be displayed in a <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaic</a> map which shows patterns of precipitation and its movement. The radar system operates in two basic modes, selectable by the operator – a slow-scanning <i>clear-air mode</i> for analyzing air movements when there is little or no activity in the area, and a <i>precipitation mode</i>, with a faster scan for tracking active weather. NEXRAD has an increased emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Automation" title="Automation">automation</a>, including the use of <a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">algorithms</a> and automated volume scans.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Deployment">Deployment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NEXRAD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Deployment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table align="right">
<tr>
<td>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NEXRAD_NETWORK.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="632" data-file-width="800" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/NEXRAD_NETWORK.jpg/220px-NEXRAD_NETWORK.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/NEXRAD_NETWORK.jpg/330px-NEXRAD_NETWORK.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/NEXRAD_NETWORK.jpg/440px-NEXRAD_NETWORK.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NEXRAD_NETWORK.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
NEXRAD sites within the Contiguous U.S.</div>
</div>
</div>
</td>
<td>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NEXRAD_NON-CONTIGUOUS.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="632" data-file-width="800" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/NEXRAD_NON-CONTIGUOUS.jpg/220px-NEXRAD_NON-CONTIGUOUS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/NEXRAD_NON-CONTIGUOUS.jpg/330px-NEXRAD_NON-CONTIGUOUS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/NEXRAD_NON-CONTIGUOUS.jpg/440px-NEXRAD_NON-CONTIGUOUS.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NEXRAD_NON-CONTIGUOUS.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
NEXRAD sites in Alaska, Hawaii, U.S. territories, and military bases.</div>
</div>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In the 1970s, the U.S. Department of Commerce, Department of Defense and Department of Transportation found the need to replace the existing national radar network, consisting of <b><a href="/wiki/WSR-74" title="WSR-74">WSR-74</a></b> and <b><a href="/wiki/WSR-57" title="WSR-57">WSR-57</a></b> radars not utilizing <a href="/wiki/Doppler_effect" title="Doppler effect">Doppler</a> technology that were respectively developed in 1974 and 1957, to better serve their operational needs. The Joint Doppler Operational Project (JDOP) was formed in 1976 at the <a href="/wiki/National_Severe_Storms_Laboratory" title="National Severe Storms Laboratory">National Severe Storms Laboratory</a> (NSSL) to study the usefulness of using Doppler radar to identify severe and tornadic <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">thunderstorms</a>. Tests over the next three years, conducted by the National Weather Service and the Air Weather Service agency of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">U.S. Air Force</a>, found that Doppler radar provided much improved early detection of severe thunderstorms. A <a href="/wiki/Working_group" title="Working group">working group</a> that included the JDOP published a paper providing the concepts for the development and operation of a national weather radar network. In 1979, the NEXRAD Joint System Program Office (JSPO) was formed to move forward with the development and deployment of the proposed NEXRAD radar network. That year, the NSSL completed a formal report on developing the NEXRAD system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsr-88d_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-wsr-88d-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stormwarning1_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-stormwarning1-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>When the proposal was presented to the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Ronald_Reagan" title="Presidency of Ronald Reagan">Reagan administration</a>, options were considered to build the radar systems: either allowing corporate bids to build the systems based on the schematics of the previously developed prototype radar or seek <a href="/wiki/Independent_contractor" title="Independent contractor">contractors</a> to build their own systems using predetermined specifications. The JSPO group opted to select a contractor to develop and produce the radars that would be used for the national network. Radar systems developed by <a href="/wiki/Raytheon" title="Raytheon">Raytheon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unisys" title="Unisys">Unisys</a> were tested during the 1980s; however, it took four years to allow the prospective contractors to develop their proprietary models. Unisys was selected as the contractor, and was awarded a full-scale production contract in January 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsr-88d_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-wsr-88d-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stormwarning1_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-stormwarning1-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Installation of an operational prototype was completed in the fall of 1990 in <a href="/wiki/Norman,_Oklahoma" title="Norman, Oklahoma">Norman, Oklahoma</a>. The first installation of a WSR-88D for operational use in everyday forecasting was in <a href="/wiki/Sterling,_Virginia" title="Sterling, Virginia">Sterling, Virginia</a> on June 12, 1992. The last system deployed as part of this installation campaign was installed in <a href="/wiki/North_Webster,_Indiana" title="North Webster, Indiana">North Webster, Indiana</a> on August 30, 1997. In 2011, a single new radar was added at Langley Hill, Washington to better cover the Pacific Coast of that area; other radars also filled gaps in coverage at <a href="/wiki/Evansville,_Indiana" title="Evansville, Indiana">Evansville, Indiana</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ft._Smith,_Arkansas" title="Ft. Smith, Arkansas">Ft. Smith, Arkansas</a> following the initial installations.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The site locations were strategically chosen to provide the most overlapping coverage between radars in case one failed during a <a href="/wiki/Severe_weather" title="Severe weather">severe weather</a> event. Where possible, they were co-located with NWS Weather Forecast Offices (WFOs) to permit quicker access to maintenance technicians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-installation_dates_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-installation_dates-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The NEXRAD radars incorporated a number of improvements over the radar systems that were previously in use. The new system provided Doppler velocity, improving <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornado</a> prediction ability by detecting any rotation present within the storm at different scan angles. It provided improved resolution and sensitivity, allowing operators to see features such as <a href="/wiki/Cold_front" title="Cold front">cold fronts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">thunderstorm</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gust_front" title="Gust front">gust fronts</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mesoscale_meteorology" title="Mesoscale meteorology">mesoscale</a> to even <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Storm_scale" title="Storm scale">storm scale</a> features of thunderstorms that had never been visible on radar. The NEXRAD radars also provided volumetric scans of the atmosphere allowing operators to interrogate the vertical structure of storms and additionally can act as <a href="/wiki/Wind_profiler" title="Wind profiler">wind profilers</a> in providing detailed wind information for several kilometers above the radar site. The radars also had a much increased range allowing detection of weather features at much greater distances from the radar site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>WSR-88D development, maintenance, and training are coordinated by the NEXRAD <a href="/wiki/Radar_Operations_Center" title="Radar Operations Center">Radar Operations Center</a> (ROC) located at the <a href="/wiki/National_Weather_Center" title="National Weather Center">National Weather Center</a> (NWC) in Norman, Oklahoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Radar_properties">Radar properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NEXRAD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Radar properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A standard WSR-88D operates at a frequency of around 2800 MHz, with a typical gain around 53 dB using a center-fed parabolic antenna. The PRF varies from 318 to 1300 Hz with a maximum power output of 700kW at Klystron output, although dependent on the volume coverage pattern (VCP) selected by the operator. All NEXRADs have a dish diameter of 9.1m (30ft) and an antenna diameter of 8.5m (28ft). Using the predetermined VCPs, NEXRADs have a traditional elevation minimum and maximum ranging from 0.1 to 19.5 degrees, although the non-operational minimum and maximum spans from -1 to +45 degrees. Unlike its predecessor, the <a href="/wiki/WSR-74" title="WSR-74">WSR-74</a>, the antenna can not be manually steered by the operator. Spatial resolution varies with data type and scan angle - level III data has a resolution of 1km x 1 degree in azimuth, while super-res level II, (implemented in 2008 nationwide), has a resolution of 250m by 0.5 degrees in azimuth below 2.4 degrees in elevation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scan_strategies">Scan strategies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NEXRAD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Scan strategies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The NEXRAD radar system continually refreshes its three-dimensional database via one of several predetermined scan patterns. These patterns have differing PRFs to fit the respective use, but all have a constant resolution. Since the system samples the atmosphere in three dimensions, there are many variables that can be changed, depending on the desired output. With all traditional VCPs, the antenna scans at a maximum of 19.5 degrees in elevation, and a minimum of .5, with some coastal sites scanning as low as .2 or lower. Due to the traditionally vertically incomplete coverage, a phenomenon known as "The Cone of Silence" is present with all NEXRAD radars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The phenomenon describes the lack of vertical radar coverage, usually directly over the site of the radar.</p>
<p>There are currently nine Volume Coverage Patterns (VCP) available to NWS meteorologists. Each VCP is a predefined set of instructions that control antenna rotation speed, elevation angle, transmitter pulse repetition frequency and pulse width. The radar operator chooses from the VCPs based on the type of weather occurring:</p>
<ul>
<li>Clear Air or Light Precipitation: VCP 31 and 32</li>
<li>Shallow Precipitation: VCP 21</li>
<li>Convection: VCP 11, 12, 121, 211, 212, and 221<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center; width: 100%; font-size: normal;">
<tr>
<th>VCP</th>
<th>Scan Time (min)</th>
<th>Elevation scans</th>
<th>Elevation angles (°)</th>
<th>Usage</th>
<th>Special attributes</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>11</td>
<td rowspan="2">5</td>
<td rowspan="2">14</td>
<td rowspan="2">0.5, 1.5, 2.4, 3.4, 4.3, 5.3, 6.2, 7.5, 8.7, 10, 12, 14, 16.7, 19.5</td>
<td>Convection, especially when close to the radar</td>
<td>Has the best overall volume coverage</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>211</td>
<td>Convection, especially when close to the radar</td>
<td>Improves range-obscured velocity data over VCP 11</td>
</tr>
<tr>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Upcoming_2018_VCP_changes">Upcoming 2018 VCP changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NEXRAD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Upcoming 2018 VCP changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In October 2015, the National Weather Service announced<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> that an upcoming software update would eliminate multiple precipitation mode VCPs, and replace them with a single VCP intended to combine the best features of the VCPs being eliminated. An additional clear air mode will also be made available.<a class="external autonumber" href="http://www.roc.noaa.gov/WSR88D/PublicDocs/NewTechnology/New_VCP_Paradigm_Public_Oct_2015.pdf" rel="nofollow">[1]</a></p>
<p>As of the build 18 update, scheduled for deployment in mid-october<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> of 2018, the following VCPs will be available:</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Enhancements">Enhancements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NEXRAD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Enhancements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Super_resolution">Super resolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NEXRAD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Super resolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Deployed from March to August 2008 with all level II data,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> the Super Resolution upgrade permitted the capability of the radar to produce much higher resolution data. Under legacy resolution, the WSR-88D provides reflectivity data at 1 km (0.62 mi) by 1 degree to 460 km (290 mi) range, and velocity data at 0.25 km (0.16 mi) by 1 degree to a range of 230 km (140 mi). Super Resolution provides reflectivity data with a sample size of 0.25 km (0.16 mi) by 0.5 degree, and increase the range of Doppler velocity data to 300 km (190 mi). Initially, the increased resolution is only available in the lower scan elevations. Super resolution makes a compromise of slightly decreased <a href="/wiki/Noise_reduction" title="Noise reduction">noise reduction</a> for a large gain in resolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>The improvement in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Azimuthal" title="Azimuthal">azimuthal</a> resolution increases the range at which tornadic mesoscale rotations can be detected. This allows for faster lead time on warnings and extends the useful range of the radar. The increased resolution (in both azimuth and range) increases the detail of such rotations, giving a more accurate representation of the storm. Along with providing better detail of detected precipitation and other mesoscale features, Super Resolution also provides additional detail to aid in other severe storm analysis. Super Resolution extends the range of velocity data and provides it faster than before, also allowing for faster lead time on potential tornado detection and subsequent warnings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dual_polarization">Dual polarization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NEXRAD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Dual polarization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table align="right">
<tr>
<td>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Non-Polarimetric_Radar.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="350" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Non-Polarimetric_Radar.gif/220px-Non-Polarimetric_Radar.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Non-Polarimetric_Radar.gif/330px-Non-Polarimetric_Radar.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/65/Non-Polarimetric_Radar.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Non-Polarimetric_Radar.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Non-Polarimetric Radar</div>
</div>
</div>
</td>
<td>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polarimetric_Radar.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="350" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Polarimetric_Radar.gif/220px-Polarimetric_Radar.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Polarimetric_Radar.gif/330px-Polarimetric_Radar.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6d/Polarimetric_Radar.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polarimetric_Radar.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Polarimetric Radar</div>
</div>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>WSR-88D sites across the nation have been upgraded to <a href="/wiki/Weather_radar#Polarization" title="Weather radar">polarimetric radar</a>, which adds vertical <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">polarization</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_plane" title="Horizontal plane">horizontal</a> <a href="/wiki/Wave" title="Wave">radar waves</a>, in order to more accurately discern what is reflecting the signal. This so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dual_polarization" title="Dual polarization">dual polarization</a> allows the radar to distinguish between <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">hail</a> and <a href="/wiki/Snow" title="Snow">snow</a>, something the horizontally polarized radars cannot accurately do. Early trials showed that rain, <a href="/wiki/Ice_pellets" title="Ice pellets">ice pellets</a>, snow, hail, <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ground_clutter" title="Ground clutter">ground clutter</a> all have different signatures with dual polarization, which could mark a significant improvement in forecasting <a href="/wiki/Winter_storm" title="Winter storm">winter storms</a> and severe thunderstorms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The deployment of the dual polarization capability (Build 12) to NEXRAD sites began in 2010 and was completed by the summer of 2013. The radar at <a href="/wiki/Vance_Air_Force_Base" title="Vance Air Force Base">Vance Air Force Base</a> in <a href="/wiki/Enid,_Oklahoma" title="Enid, Oklahoma">Enid, Oklahoma</a> is the first operational WSR-88D to be modified to utilize dual polarization technology; the modified radar went into operation on March 3, 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="AVSET">AVSET</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NEXRAD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: AVSET">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When the NEXRAD system was initially implemented, the radar automatically scanned all scan angles in a Volume Coverage Pattern, even if the highest scan angles were free of precipitation. As a result, in many cases when severe weather was further from the radar site, forecasters could not provide as timely severe weather warnings as possible. The Automated Volume Scan Evaluation and Termination (AVSET) algorithm<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> helps solve this problem by immediately ending the volume scan when precipitation returns at higher scan angles drop below a set threshold (around 20 dBZ). This can often allow for more volume scans per hour, improving severe weather detection without the need for hardware upgrades<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> AVSET was initially deployed in RPG build 12.3, in Fall of 2011.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="SAILS_and_MESO-SAILS">SAILS and MESO-SAILS</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NEXRAD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: SAILS and MESO-SAILS">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/MESO-SAILS" title="MESO-SAILS">MESO-SAILS</a></div>
<p>One of the primary weaknesses of the WSR-88D radar system was the lack of frequency of base (0.5 degree) scans, especially during severe weather. Forecasters, and TV viewers at home, often had access to images that were four or five minutes old, and therefore had inaccurate information. TV viewers at home could be lulled into a false sense of security that a tornado was farther away from them than it really was, endangering residents in the storm's path. The Supplemental Adaptive Intra-Volume Low-Level Scan (SAILS) technique, deployed with Build 14 in the first half of 2014, allows operators the option to run an additional base scan during the middle of a typical volume scan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> With one SAILS cut active on VCP 212, base scans occur about once every two and a half minutes, with more frequent updates if AVSET terminates the volume scan early.</p>
<p>Multiple Elevation Scan Option for Supplemental Adaptive Intra-Volume Low-Level Scan (MESO-SAILS) is an enhancement to SAILS, which allows the radar operator to run one, two or three additional base scans during the course of a volume scan, per the operators request.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-11">[11]</a></sup> During June 2013, the Radar Operations Center first tested SAILSx2, which adds two additional low-level scans per volume. It was executed for approximately 4 and 1/2 hours and during the testing, a Radar Technician observed the pedestal/antenna assembly's behavior. No excessive wear was noted to the assembly of the Radar. Two days later, SAILSx3 was executed, which added 3 additional low-level scans to a volume. During this 1 1/2 hour test of SAILSx3, a ROC Radar Hardware Engineer accompanied the ROC Electronics Maintenance Technician to observe the antenna/pedestal assembly. Again, no excessive wear was noted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> MESO-SAILS was deployed with Build 16.1, in spring of 2016.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="MRLE">MRLE</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NEXRAD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: MRLE">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/M.R.L.E." title="M.R.L.E.">M.R.L.E.</a></div>
<p>During Quasi-Linear Convective Systems (QLCS), colloquially known as <a href="/wiki/Squall_line" title="Squall line">squall lines</a>, the detection of <a href="/wiki/Mesovortices" title="Mesovortices">mesovortices</a>, which generate at 4,000 to 8,000 feet above ground level,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> is not always possible with SAILS cuts, as the base 0.5 degree scan travels below the formation of mesovortices at closer distances to the radar. Mid-Volume Rescan of Low-Level Elevations (MRLE) consecutively scans either the two, three or four lowest scan angles during the middle of a typical volume scan, allowing more frequent surveillance of mesovortex formation during QLCS events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> MRLE will be deployed on a non-operational basis in RPG 18.0 in Fall of 2017, with possible operational deployment with RPG 19.0, if proven useful or of importance.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Service_Life_Extension_Program">Service Life Extension Program</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NEXRAD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Service Life Extension Program">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Started on March 13, 2013, the SLEP, or Service Life Extension Program, is an extensive effort to keep and maintain the current NEXRAD network in working order for as long as possible. These improvements include Signal Processor upgrades, Pedestal upgrades, Transmitter upgrades, and shelter upgrades. The program is anticipated to be finished by 2022, which coincides with the beginnings of a nationwide implementation of Multi-function Phased Array Radars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coverage_gaps">Coverage gaps</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NEXRAD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Coverage gaps">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WSR-88DCONUSCoverage1000.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="773" data-file-width="1000" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/WSR-88DCONUSCoverage1000.jpg/220px-WSR-88DCONUSCoverage1000.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/WSR-88DCONUSCoverage1000.jpg/330px-WSR-88DCONUSCoverage1000.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/WSR-88DCONUSCoverage1000.jpg/440px-WSR-88DCONUSCoverage1000.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WSR-88DCONUSCoverage1000.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
NEXRAD coverage below 10,000 feet</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>WSR-88D has coverage gaps below 10,000 feet (or no coverage at all) in many parts of the continental United States, often for terrain or budgetary reasons, or remoteness of the area. Such notable gaps include most of <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>; several areas of <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, including the central and southern coast and much of the area east of the Cascade Mountains; many portions of the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a>; <a href="/wiki/Pierre,_South_Dakota" title="Pierre, South Dakota">Pierre, South Dakota</a>; portions of <a href="/wiki/North_Texas" title="North Texas">northern Texas</a>; large portions of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nebraska_panhandle" title="Nebraska panhandle">Nebraska panhandle</a>; and areas near the borders of the <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma_Panhandle" title="Oklahoma Panhandle">Oklahoma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Texas_Panhandle" title="Texas Panhandle">Texas Panhandles</a>. Notably, many of these gaps lie in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tornado_alley" title="Tornado alley">tornado alley</a>. At least one tornado has gone undetected by WSR-88D as a result of such a coverage gap – an EF1 tornado in <a href="/wiki/Lovelady,_Texas" title="Lovelady, Texas">Lovelady, Texas</a> in April 2014. As a result of the coverage gap, initial reports of tornadic activity were treated with skepticism by the local National Weather Service forecast office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nick_Wiltgen_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nick_Wiltgen-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Coverage gaps can also be caused during radar outages, especially in areas with little to no overlapping coverage. A radar failure on July 16, 2013 resulted in an outage that lasted through early August<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>A coverage gap in North Carolina encouraged <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Senator_Richard_Burr" title="Senator Richard Burr">Senator Richard Burr</a> to propose S. 2058, also known as the Metropolitan Weather Hazard Protection Act of 2015. The act mandates that any city with a population of 700,000 or more must have Doppler Radar coverage &lt;6,000 feet above ground level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The bill passed the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a>, but died in a <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House</a> committee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is not likely that additional WSR-88Ds will be deployed, as the production line was shut down in 1997, and the National Weather Service has an insufficient budget to restart production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nick_Wiltgen_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nick_Wiltgen-26">[26]</a></sup> In 2011, a known coverage gap was filled when the Langley Hill radar in southwestern Washington was installed, using the last remaining spare. This radar opportunity was spearheaded by a public campaign led by Prof. Clifford Mass at the University of Washington, and likely helped the NWS office in Portland to issue a timely warning for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2016_Manzanita_tornado" title="2016 Manzanita tornado">Manzanita, OR EF-2 tornado</a> in October, 2016.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_enhancements">Future enhancements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NEXRAD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Future enhancements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_NEXRAD_system">Current NEXRAD system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NEXRAD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Current NEXRAD system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The National Weather Service keeps a list of upcoming improvements to the WSR-88D system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Multi-function_Phased_Array_Radar_(MPAR)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Multi-function_Phased_Array_Radar_.28MPAR.29">Multi-function Phased Array Radar (MPAR)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NEXRAD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Multi-function Phased Array Radar (MPAR)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Multifunction_Phased_Array_Radar" title="Multifunction Phased Array Radar">Multifunction Phased Array Radar</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Par_installation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="432" data-file-width="288" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Par_installation.jpg/140px-Par_installation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Par_installation.jpg/210px-Par_installation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Par_installation.jpg/280px-Par_installation.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Par_installation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Multi-Function Phased Array Radar during installation in Norman, Oklahoma, 2003</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Beyond dual-polarization, the advent of <a href="/wiki/Phased_array" title="Phased array">phased array</a> radar will probably be the next major improvement in severe weather detection. Its ability to rapidly scan large areas would give an enormous advantage to radar meteorologists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-31">[31]</a></sup> Its additional ability to track both known and unknown aircraft in three dimensions would allow a phased array network to simultaneously replace the current <a href="/wiki/Air_Route_Surveillance_Radar" title="Air Route Surveillance Radar">Air Route Surveillance Radar</a> network, saving the United States government billions of dollars in maintenance costs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Any large-scale installation by the NWS and Department of Defense is unlikely to occur before 2020. The National Severe Storms Laboratory predicts that a phased array system will eventually replace the current network of WSR-88D radar transmitters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NEXRAD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Usage">Usage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NEXRAD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Usage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>NEXRAD data is used in multiple ways. It is used by National Weather Service meteorologists and (under <a href="/wiki/Copyright_status_of_work_by_the_U.S._government" title="Copyright status of work by the U.S. government">provisions of U.S. law</a>) is <a href="/wiki/Public_domain" title="Public domain">freely available</a> to users outside of the NWS, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Researchers" title="Researchers">researchers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mass_media" title="Mass media">media</a>, and private <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Citizens" title="Citizens">citizens</a>. The primary goal of NEXRAD data is to aid NWS meteorologists in operational <a href="/wiki/Forecasting" title="Forecasting">forecasting</a>. The data allows them to accurately track precipitation and anticipate its development and track. More importantly, it allows the meteorologists to track and anticipate severe weather and tornadoes. Combined with ground reports, <a href="/wiki/Tornado_warning" title="Tornado warning">tornado</a> and <a href="/wiki/Severe_thunderstorm_warning" title="Severe thunderstorm warning">severe thunderstorm</a> warnings can be issued to alert the public about dangerous storms. NEXRAD data also provides information about rainfall rate and aids in <a href="/wiki/Hydrology" title="Hydrology">hydrological</a> forecasting. Data is provided to the public in several different forms, the most basic form being graphics published to the NWS website. Data is also available in two similar, but different, raw formats. Available directly from the NWS is Level III data, consisting of reduced resolution, low-<a href="/wiki/Bandwidth_(signal_processing)" title="Bandwidth (signal processing)">bandwidth</a> base products as well as many derived, post-processed products; Level II data consists of only the base products, but at their original resolution. Because of the higher bandwidth costs, Level II data is not available directly from the NWS. The NWS distributes this data freely to <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Web_Services" title="Amazon Web Services">Amazon Web Services</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> and several top-tier <a href="/wiki/University" title="University">universities</a>, which in turn distribute the data to private organizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>

Battan">Battan Memorial</a> and 40th Anniversary Radar Meteorology Conference</i>, published by the <a href="/wiki/American_Meteorological_Society" title="American Meteorological Society">American Meteorological Society</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>, 1990, 806 pages, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-933876-86-6" title="Special:BookSources/0-933876-86-6">0-933876-86-6</a>, AMS Code RADMET.</li>
